Title: Evolution

URL Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution

Published Time: 2001-11-06T00:39:57Z

Markdown Content:
**Evolution** is the change in the [heritable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heritable "Heritable")[characteristics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotypic_trait "Phenotypic trait") of biological populations over successive generations.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-1)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-2) It occurs when evolutionary processes such as [natural selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_selection "Natural selection") and [genetic drift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_drift "Genetic drift") act on genetic variation, resulting in certain characteristics becoming more or less common within a population over successive generations.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Scott-Phillips-2014-3) The process of evolution has given rise to [biodiversity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biodiversity "Biodiversity") at every level of [biological organisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_organisation "Biological organisation").[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-4)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Voet-2016-5)

The [scientific theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_theory "Scientific theory") of evolution by natural selection was conceived independently by two British naturalists, [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin") and [Alfred Russel Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace "Alfred Russel Wallace"), in the mid-19th century as an explanation for why organisms are adapted to their physical and biological environments. The theory was first set out in detail in Darwin's book _[On the Origin of Species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On\_the\_Origin\_of\_Species "On the Origin of Species")_.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-6) Evolution by natural selection is established by observable facts about living organisms: (1) more offspring are often produced than can possibly survive; (2) [traits vary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotypic_variation "Phenotypic variation") among individuals with respect to their [morphology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)"), [physiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physiology "Physiology"), and behaviour; (3) different traits confer different rates of survival and reproduction (differential [fitness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fitness_(biology) "Fitness (biology)")); and (4) traits can be passed from generation to generation ([heritability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heritability "Heritability") of fitness).[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Lewontin-1970-7) In successive generations, members of a population are therefore more likely to be replaced by the [offspring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Offspring "Offspring") of parents with favourable characteristics for that environment.

In the early 20th century, [competing ideas of evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternatives_to_evolution_by_natural_selection "Alternatives to evolution by natural selection") were [refuted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superseded_theories_in_science "Superseded theories in science") and evolution was combined with [Mendelian inheritance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance "Mendelian inheritance") and [population genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_genetics "Population genetics") to give rise to modern evolutionary theory.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma2017a-8)[In this synthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_synthesis_(20th_century) "Modern synthesis (20th century)") the basis for heredity is in [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") molecules that pass information from generation to generation. The processes that change DNA in a population include natural selection, genetic drift, [mutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation "Mutation"), and [gene flow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_flow "Gene flow").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Scott-Phillips-2014-3)

All life on Earth—including [humanity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution "Human evolution")—shares a [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") (LUCA),[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kampourakis-2014-9)[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Doolittle-2000-10)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-11) which lived approximately 3.5–3.8 billion years ago.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Schopf-2007-12) The [fossil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil "Fossil") record includes a progression from early [biogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogenic "Biogenic")[graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite")[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ohtomo-2014-13) to [microbial mat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbial_mat "Microbial mat") fossils[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Borenstein-2013-14)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Pearlman-2013-15)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Noffke-2013-16) to fossilised [multicellular organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multicellular_organism "Multicellular organism"). Existing patterns of biodiversity have been shaped by repeated formations of new species ([speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speciation "Speciation")), changes within species ([anagenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anagenesis "Anagenesis")), and loss of species ([extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction "Extinction")) throughout the evolutionary [history of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_life "History of life") on Earth.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma04-17)[Morphological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)") and [biochemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biochemical "Biochemical") traits tend to be more similar among species that share a more [recent common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recent_common_ancestor "Recent common ancestor"), which historically was used to reconstruct [phylogenetic trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree "Phylogenetic tree"), although direct comparison of genetic sequences is a more common method today.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Panno_2005-18)[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-19)

[Evolutionary biologists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_biologists "Evolutionary biologists") have continued to study various aspects of evolution by forming and testing [hypotheses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypotheses "Hypotheses") as well as constructing theories based on [evidence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical_evidence "Empirical evidence") from the field or laboratory and on data generated by the methods of [mathematical and theoretical biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_and_theoretical_biology "Mathematical and theoretical biology"). Their discoveries have influenced not just the development of [biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology "Biology") but also other fields including agriculture, medicine, and [computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science").[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma-1999-20)

Heredity
--------

[![Image 1](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/ADN_static.png/250px-ADN_static.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ADN_static.png)

[DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") structure. [Bases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleobase "Nucleobase") are in the centre, surrounded by phosphate–sugar chains in a [double helix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_helix "Double helix").

Evolution in organisms occurs through changes in heritable characteristics—the inherited characteristics of an organism. In humans, for example, [eye colour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eye_colour "Eye colour") is an inherited characteristic and an individual might inherit the "brown-eye trait" from one of their parents.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-21) Inherited traits are controlled by genes and the complete set of genes within an organism's [genome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome "Genome") (genetic material) is called its _[genotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genotype "Genotype")_.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Pearson-2006-22)

The complete set of observable traits that make up the structure and behaviour of an organism is called its _[phenotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotype "Phenotype")_. Some of these traits come from the interaction of its genotype with the environment while others are neutral.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-23) Some observable characteristics are not inherited. For example, [suntanned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suntanned "Suntanned") skin comes from the interaction between a person's genotype and sunlight; thus, suntans are not passed on to people's children. The phenotype is the ability of the skin to tan when exposed to sunlight. However, some people tan more easily than others, due to differences in genotypic variation; a striking example are people with the inherited trait of [albinism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albinism "Albinism"), who do not tan at all and are very sensitive to [sunburn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunburn "Sunburn").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-24)

Heritable characteristics are passed from one generation to the next via [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA"), a [molecule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecule "Molecule") that encodes genetic information.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Pearson-2006-22) DNA is a long [biopolymer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biopolymer "Biopolymer") composed of four types of bases. The sequence of bases along a particular DNA molecule specifies the genetic information, in a manner similar to a sequence of letters spelling out a sentence. Before a cell divides, the DNA is copied, so that each of the resulting two cells will inherit the DNA sequence. Portions of a DNA molecule that specify a single functional unit are called genes; different genes have different sequences of bases. Within cells, each long strand of DNA is called a [chromosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromosome "Chromosome"). The specific location of a DNA sequence within a chromosome is known as a [locus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locus_(genetics) "Locus (genetics)"). If the DNA sequence at a locus varies between individuals, the different forms of this sequence are called alleles. DNA sequences can change through mutations, producing new alleles. If a mutation occurs within a gene, the new allele may affect the trait that the gene controls, altering the phenotype of the organism.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma_2005-25) However, while this simple correspondence between an allele and a trait works in some cases, most traits are influenced by multiple genes in a [quantitative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantitative_trait_loci "Quantitative trait loci") or [epistatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epistatic "Epistatic") manner.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-26)[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Min_Lin-2006-27)

Sources of variation
--------------------

[![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Biston.betularia.7200.jpg/250px-Biston.betularia.7200.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Biston.betularia.7200.jpg)

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Biston.betularia.f.carbonaria.7209.jpg/250px-Biston.betularia.f.carbonaria.7209.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Biston.betularia.f.carbonaria.7209.jpg)

Evolution can occur if there is genetic variation within a population. Variation comes from mutations in the genome, reshuffling of genes through [sexual reproduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_reproduction "Sexual reproduction") and migration between populations ([gene flow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_flow "Gene flow")). Despite the constant introduction of new variation through mutation and gene flow, most of the genome of a species is very similar among all individuals of that species.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-28) However, discoveries in the field of [evolutionary developmental biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology "Evolutionary developmental biology") have demonstrated that even relatively small differences in genotype can lead to dramatic differences in phenotype both within and between species.

An individual organism's phenotype results from both its genotype and the influence of the environment it has lived in.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Min_Lin-2006-27) The modern evolutionary synthesis defines evolution as the change over time in this genetic variation. The frequency of one particular allele will become more or less prevalent relative to other forms of that gene. Variation disappears when a new allele reaches the point of [fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fixation_(population_genetics) "Fixation (population genetics)")—when it either disappears from the population or replaces the ancestral allele entirely.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Amos-1998-29)

### Mutation

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Gene-duplication.svg/190px-Gene-duplication.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gene-duplication.svg)

Duplication of part of a [chromosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromosome "Chromosome")

Mutations are changes in the DNA sequence of a cell's genome and are the ultimate source of genetic variation in all organisms.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma2017c-30) When mutations occur, they may alter the [product of a gene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_product "Gene product"), or prevent the gene from functioning, or have no effect.

About half of the mutations in the coding regions of protein-coding genes are deleterious — the other half are neutral. A small percentage of the total mutations in this region confer a fitness benefit.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-31) Some of the mutations in other parts of the genome are deleterious but the vast majority are neutral. A few are beneficial.

Mutations can involve large sections of a chromosome becoming [duplicated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_duplication "Gene duplication") (usually by [genetic recombination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_recombination "Genetic recombination")), which can introduce extra copies of a gene into a genome.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-32) Extra copies of genes are a major source of the raw material needed for new genes to evolve.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-33) This is important because most new genes evolve within [gene families](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_families "Gene families") from pre-existing genes that share common ancestors.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-34) For example, the [human eye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_eye "Human eye") uses four genes to make structures that sense light: three for [colour vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cone_cell "Cone cell") and one for [night vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rod_cell "Rod cell"); all four are descended from a single ancestral gene.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-35)

New genes can be generated from an ancestral gene when a duplicate copy mutates and acquires a new function. This process is easier once a gene has been duplicated because it increases the [redundancy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_redundancy "Gene redundancy") of the system; one gene in the pair can acquire a new function while the other copy continues to perform its original function.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-36)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-37) Other types of mutations can even generate entirely new genes from previously noncoding DNA, a phenomenon termed [_de novo_ gene birth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_novo_gene_birth "De novo gene birth").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-38)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-39)

The generation of new genes can also involve small parts of several genes being duplicated, with these fragments then recombining to form new combinations with new functions ([exon shuffling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exon_shuffling "Exon shuffling")).[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-40)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-41) When new genes are assembled from shuffling pre-existing parts, [domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_domain "Protein domain") act as modules with simple independent functions, which can be mixed together to produce new combinations with new and complex functions.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-42) For example, [polyketide synthases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyketide_synthase "Polyketide synthase") are large [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme") that make [antibiotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic "Antibiotic"); they contain up to 100 independent domains that each catalyse one step in the overall process, like a step in an assembly line.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-43)

One example of mutation is [wild boar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wild_boar "Wild boar") piglets. They are camouflage coloured and show a characteristic pattern of dark and light longitudinal stripes. However, mutations in the _[melanocortin 1 receptor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanocortin\_1\_receptor "Melanocortin 1 receptor")_ (_MC1R_) disrupt the pattern. The majority of pig breeds carry MC1R mutations disrupting wild-type colour and different mutations causing dominant black colouring.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-44)

### Sex and recombination

In [asexual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asexual_reproduction "Asexual reproduction") organisms, genes are inherited together, or _linked_, as they cannot mix with genes of other organisms during reproduction. In contrast, the offspring of sexual organisms contain random mixtures of their parents' chromosomes that are produced through independent assortment. In a related process called [homologous recombination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homologous_recombination "Homologous recombination"), sexual organisms exchange DNA between two matching chromosomes.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-45) Recombination and reassortment do not alter allele frequencies, but instead change which alleles are associated with each other, producing offspring with new combinations of alleles.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Agrawal-2006-46) Sex usually increases genetic variation and may increase the rate of evolution.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-47)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-48)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Evolsex-dia1a.svg/330px-Evolsex-dia1a.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Evolsex-dia1a.svg)

This diagram illustrates the _twofold cost of sex_. If each individual were to contribute to the same number of offspring (two), _(a)_ the sexual population remains the same size each generation, where the _(b)_[Asexual reproduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asexual_reproduction "Asexual reproduction") population doubles in size each generation.[_[image reference needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

The two-fold cost of sex was first described by [John Maynard Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Maynard_Smith "John Maynard Smith").[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-maynard-49) The first cost is that in sexually dimorphic species only one of the two sexes can bear young. This cost does not apply to hermaphroditic species, like most plants and many [invertebrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrate "Invertebrate"). The second cost is that any individual who reproduces sexually can only pass on 50% of its genes to any individual offspring, with even less passed on as each new generation passes.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-ridley-50) Yet sexual reproduction is the more common means of reproduction among eukaryotes and multicellular organisms. The [Red Queen hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Queen_hypothesis "Red Queen hypothesis") has been used to explain the significance of sexual reproduction as a means to enable continual evolution and adaptation in response to [coevolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coevolution "Coevolution") with other species in an ever-changing environment.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-ridley-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Van_Valen-1973-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hamilton-1990-52)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Birdsell-53) Another hypothesis is that sexual reproduction is primarily an adaptation for promoting accurate recombinational repair of damage in germline DNA, and that increased diversity is a byproduct of this process that may sometimes be adaptively beneficial.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-54)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-55)

### Gene flow

Gene flow is the exchange of genes between populations and between species.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Morjan-2004-56) It can therefore be a source of variation that is new to a population or to a species. Gene flow can be caused by the movement of individuals between separate populations of organisms, as might be caused by the movement of mice between inland and coastal populations, or the movement of [pollen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pollen "Pollen") between heavy-metal-tolerant and heavy-metal-sensitive populations of grasses.

Gene transfer between species includes the formation of [hybrid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology) "Hybrid (biology)") organisms and [horizontal gene transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer "Horizontal gene transfer"). Horizontal gene transfer is the transfer of genetic material from one organism to another organism that is not its offspring; this is most common among bacteria.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-57) In medicine, this contributes to the spread of [antibiotic resistance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance "Antibiotic resistance"), as when one bacteria acquires resistance genes it can rapidly transfer them to other species.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Walsh-2006-58) Horizontal transfer of genes from bacteria to eukaryotes such as the yeast _[Saccharomyces cerevisiae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saccharomyces\_cerevisiae "Saccharomyces cerevisiae")_ and the adzuki bean weevil _[Callosobruchus chinensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Callosobruchus\_chinensis "Callosobruchus chinensis")_ has occurred.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-59)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-60) An example of larger-scale transfers are the eukaryotic [bdelloid rotifers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bdelloid_rotifers "Bdelloid rotifers"), which have received a range of genes from bacteria, fungi and plants.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-61) Viruses can also carry DNA between organisms, allowing transfer of genes even across [biological domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(biology) "Domain (biology)").[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-62)

Large-scale gene transfer has also occurred between the ancestors of [eukaryotic cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryotic_cell "Eukaryotic cell") and bacteria, during the acquisition of [chloroplasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloroplast "Chloroplast") and [mitochondria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondria "Mitochondria"). It is possible that eukaryotes themselves originated from horizontal gene transfers between bacteria and [archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea").[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-63)

### Epigenetics

Some heritable changes cannot be explained by changes to the sequence of [nucleotides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleotide "Nucleotide") in the DNA. These phenomena are classed as epigenetic inheritance systems.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Jablonka-2009-64)[DNA methylation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_methylation "DNA methylation") marking [chromatin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromatin "Chromatin"), self-sustaining metabolic loops, gene silencing by [RNA interference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_interference "RNA interference") and the three-dimensional [conformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_structure "Protein structure") of [proteins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein") (such as [prions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prion "Prion")) are areas where epigenetic inheritance systems have been discovered at the organismic level.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bossdorf-2010-65) Developmental biologists suggest that complex interactions in [genetic networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_networks "Genetic networks") and communication among cells can lead to heritable variations that may underlay some of the mechanics in [developmental plasticity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developmental_plasticity "Developmental plasticity") and [canalisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canalisation_(genetics) "Canalisation (genetics)").[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Jablonka-2002-66) Heritability may also occur at even larger scales. For example, ecological inheritance through the process of [niche construction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niche_construction "Niche construction") is defined by the regular and repeated activities of organisms in their environment. This generates a legacy of effects that modify and feed back into the selection regime of subsequent generations.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Laland-2006-67) Other examples of heritability in evolution that are not under the direct control of genes include the inheritance of cultural traits and [symbiogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiogenesis "Symbiogenesis").[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Chapman-1998-68)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Wilson-2007-69)

Evolutionary forces
-------------------

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mutation_and_selection_diagram.svg/340px-Mutation_and_selection_diagram.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mutation_and_selection_diagram.svg)

[Mutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation "Mutation") followed by natural selection results in a population with darker colouration.

From a [neo-Darwinian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neo-Darwinian "Neo-Darwinian") perspective, evolution occurs when there are changes in the frequencies of alleles within a population of interbreeding organisms,[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ewens_W.J._2004-70) for example, the allele for black colour in a population of moths becoming more common. Mechanisms that can lead to changes in allele frequencies include natural selection, genetic drift, and mutation bias.

### Natural selection

Evolution by natural selection is the process by which traits that enhance survival and reproduction become more common in successive generations of a population. It embodies three principles:[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Lewontin-1970-7)

*   Variation exists within populations of organisms with respect to morphology, physiology and behaviour (phenotypic variation).
*   Different traits confer different rates of survival and reproduction (differential fitness).
*   These traits can be passed from generation to generation (heritability of fitness).

More offspring are produced than can possibly survive, and these conditions produce competition between organisms for survival and reproduction. Consequently, organisms with traits that give them an advantage over their competitors are more likely to pass on their traits to the next generation than those with traits that do not confer an advantage.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hurst-2009-71) This [teleonomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleonomy "Teleonomy") is the quality whereby the process of natural selection creates and preserves traits that are [seemingly fitted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleology_in_biology "Teleology in biology") for the [functional](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(biology) "Function (biology)") roles they perform.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-72) Consequences of selection include [nonrandom mating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assortative_mating "Assortative mating")[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-73) and [genetic hitchhiking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_hitchhiking "Genetic hitchhiking").

The central concept of natural selection is the [evolutionary fitness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_fitness "Evolutionary fitness") of an organism.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Orr-2009-74) Fitness is measured by an organism's ability to survive and reproduce, which determines the size of its genetic contribution to the next generation.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Orr-2009-74) However, fitness is not the same as the total number of offspring: instead fitness is indicated by the proportion of subsequent generations that carry an organism's genes.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Haldane-1959-75) For example, if an organism could survive well and reproduce rapidly, but its offspring were all too small and weak to survive, this organism would make little genetic contribution to future generations and would thus have low fitness.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Orr-2009-74)

If an allele increases fitness more than the other alleles of that gene, then with each generation this allele has a higher probability of becoming common within the population. These traits are said to be selected _for_. Examples of traits that can increase fitness are enhanced survival and increased [fecundity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fecundity "Fecundity"). Conversely, the lower fitness caused by having a less beneficial or deleterious allele results in this allele likely becoming rarer—they are selected _against_.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Lande-1983-76)

Importantly, the fitness of an allele is not a fixed characteristic; if the environment changes, previously neutral or harmful traits may become beneficial and previously beneficial traits become harmful.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma_2005-25) However, even if the direction of selection does reverse in this way, traits that were lost in the past may not re-evolve in an identical form.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-77)[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-78) However, a re-activation of dormant genes, as long as they have not been eliminated from the genome and were only suppressed perhaps for hundreds of generations, can lead to the re-occurrence of traits thought to be lost like hindlegs in dolphins, teeth in chickens, wings in wingless stick insects, tails and additional nipples in humans etc. "Throwbacks" such as these are known as [atavisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atavism "Atavism").[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-79)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Genetic_Distribution.svg/500px-Genetic_Distribution.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Genetic_Distribution.svg)

These charts depict the different types of genetic selection. On each graph, the x-axis variable is the type of [phenotypic trait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotypic_trait "Phenotypic trait") and the y-axis variable is the number of organisms.[_[image reference needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_] Group A is the original population and Group B is the population after selection.

**·** Graph 1 shows [directional selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directional_selection "Directional selection"), in which a single extreme [phenotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotype "Phenotype") is favoured.

**·** Graph 2 depicts [stabilising selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stabilising_selection "Stabilising selection"), where the intermediate phenotype is favoured over the extreme traits.

**·** Graph 3 shows [disruptive selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disruptive_selection "Disruptive selection"), in which the extreme phenotypes are favoured over the intermediate.

Natural selection within a population for a trait that can vary across a range of values, such as height, can be categorised into three different types. The first is [directional selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directional_selection "Directional selection"), which is a shift in the average value of a trait over time—for example, organisms slowly getting taller.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-80) Secondly, [disruptive selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disruptive_selection "Disruptive selection") is selection for extreme trait values and often results in [two different values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bimodal_distribution "Bimodal distribution") becoming most common, with selection against the average value. This would be when either short or tall organisms had an advantage, but not those of medium height. Finally, in [stabilising selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stabilising_selection "Stabilising selection") there is selection against extreme trait values on both ends, which causes a decrease in [variance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variance "Variance") around the average value and less diversity.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hurst-2009-71)[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-81) This would, for example, cause organisms to eventually have a similar height.

Natural selection most generally makes nature the measure against which individuals and individual traits, are more or less likely to survive. "Nature" in this sense refers to an [ecosystem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem "Ecosystem"), that is, a system in which organisms interact with every other element, [physical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiotic_component "Abiotic component") as well as [biological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotic_component "Biotic component"), in their local environment. [Eugene Odum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Odum "Eugene Odum"), a founder of ecology, defined an ecosystem as: "Any unit that includes all of the organisms...in a given area interacting with the physical environment so that a flow of energy leads to clearly defined trophic structure, biotic diversity, and material cycles (i.e., exchange of materials between living and nonliving parts) within the system...."[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Odum1971-82) Each population within an ecosystem occupies a distinct [niche](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecological_niche "Ecological niche"), or position, with distinct relationships to other parts of the system. These relationships involve the life history of the organism, its position in the [food chain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_chain "Food chain") and its geographic range. This broad understanding of nature enables scientists to delineate specific forces which, together, comprise natural selection.

Natural selection can act at [different levels of organisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_selection "Unit of selection"), such as genes, cells, individual organisms, groups of organisms and species.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Okasha07-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gould-1998-84)[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Mayr-1997-85) Selection can act at multiple levels simultaneously.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-86) An example of selection occurring below the level of the individual organism are genes called [transposons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transposons "Transposons"), which can replicate and spread throughout a genome.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-87) Selection at a level above the individual, such as [group selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_selection "Group selection"), may allow the evolution of cooperation.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-88)

### Genetic drift

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Allele-frequency.png/250px-Allele-frequency.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Allele-frequency.png)

Simulation of genetic drift of 20 unlinked alleles in populations of 10 (top) and 100 (bottom). Drift to [fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fixation_(population_genetics) "Fixation (population genetics)") is more rapid in the smaller population.[_[image reference needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Genetic drift is the random fluctuation of [allele frequencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allele_frequencies "Allele frequencies") within a population from one generation to the next.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Futuyma2017b-89) When selective forces are absent or relatively weak, allele frequencies are equally likely to _drift_ upward or downward[_[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please\_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_] in each successive generation because the alleles are subject to [sampling error](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sampling_error "Sampling error").[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Masel-2011-90) This drift halts when an allele eventually becomes fixed, either by disappearing from the population or by replacing the other alleles entirely. Genetic drift may therefore eliminate some alleles from a population due to chance alone. Even in the absence of selective forces, genetic drift can cause two separate populations that begin with the same genetic structure to drift apart into two divergent populations with different sets of alleles.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-91)

According to the [neutral theory of molecular evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution "Neutral theory of molecular evolution") most evolutionary changes are the result of the fixation of [neutral mutations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_mutation "Neutral mutation") by genetic drift.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kimura-1991-92) In this model, most genetic changes in a population are thus the result of constant mutation pressure and genetic drift.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-93) This form of the neutral theory has been debated since it does not seem to fit some genetic variation seen in nature.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-94)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-95) A better-supported version of this model is the [nearly neutral theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nearly_neutral_theory "Nearly neutral theory"), according to which a mutation that would be effectively neutral in a small population is not necessarily neutral in a large population.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hurst-2009-71) Other theories propose that genetic drift is dwarfed by other [stochastic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic "Stochastic") forces in evolution, such as genetic hitchhiking, also known as genetic draft.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Masel-2011-90)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gillespie-2001-96)[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-97) Another concept is [constructive neutral evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constructive_neutral_evolution "Constructive neutral evolution") (CNE), which explains that complex systems can emerge and spread into a population through neutral transitions due to the principles of excess capacity, presuppression, and ratcheting,[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-98)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-99)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-100) and it has been applied in areas ranging from the origins of the [spliceosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spliceosome "Spliceosome") to the complex interdependence of [microbial communities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbial_consortium "Microbial consortium").[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-101)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-102)[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-103)

The time it takes a neutral allele to become fixed by genetic drift depends on population size; fixation is more rapid in smaller populations.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-104) The number of individuals in a population is not critical, but instead a measure known as the effective population size.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Charlesworth-2009-105) The effective population is usually smaller than the total population since it takes into account factors such as the level of inbreeding and the stage of the lifecycle in which the population is the smallest.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Charlesworth-2009-105) The effective population size may not be the same for every gene in the same population.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-106)

It is usually difficult to measure the relative importance of selection and neutral processes, including drift.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-107) The comparative importance of adaptive and non-adaptive forces in driving evolutionary change is an area of [current research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_biology "Evolutionary biology").[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-108)

### Mutation bias

[Mutation bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation_bias "Mutation bias") is usually conceived as a difference in expected rates for two different kinds of mutation, e.g., transition-transversion bias, GC-AT bias, deletion-insertion bias. This is related to the idea of [developmental bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developmental_bias "Developmental bias"). [J. B. S. Haldane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._B._S._Haldane "J. B. S. Haldane")[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Haldane-1927-109) and [Ronald Fisher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Fisher "Ronald Fisher")[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Fisher1930-110) argued that, because mutation is a weak pressure easily overcome by selection, tendencies of mutation would be ineffectual except under conditions of neutral evolution or extraordinarily high mutation rates. This opposing-pressures argument was long used to dismiss the possibility of internal tendencies in evolution,[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Yampolsky-2001-111) until the molecular era prompted renewed interest in neutral evolution.

Noboru Sueoka[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Sueoka-1962-112) and [Ernst Freese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Freese "Ernst Freese")[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Freese-1962-113) proposed that systematic biases in mutation might be responsible for systematic differences in genomic GC composition between species. The identification of a GC-biased _E. coli_ mutator strain in 1967,[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Cox-1967-114) along with the proposal of the [neutral theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution "Neutral theory of molecular evolution"), established the plausibility of mutational explanations for molecular patterns, which are now common in the molecular evolution literature.

For instance, mutation biases are frequently invoked in models of codon usage.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Shah-2011-115) Such models also include effects of selection, following the mutation-selection-drift model,[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bulmer-1991-116) which allows both for mutation biases and differential selection based on effects on translation. Hypotheses of mutation bias have played an important role in the development of thinking about the evolution of genome composition, including isochores.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Fryxell-2000-117) Different insertion vs. deletion biases in different [taxa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxa "Taxa") can lead to the evolution of different genome sizes.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-118)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-119) The hypothesis of Lynch regarding genome size relies on mutational biases toward increase or decrease in genome size.

However, mutational hypotheses for the evolution of composition suffered a reduction in scope when it was discovered that (1) GC-biased gene conversion makes an important contribution to composition in diploid organisms such as mammals[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Duret-2009-120) and (2) bacterial genomes frequently have AT-biased mutation.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hershberg-2010-121)

Contemporary thinking about the role of mutation biases reflects a different theory from that of Haldane and Fisher. More recent work[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Yampolsky-2001-111) showed that the original "pressures" theory assumes that evolution is based on standing variation: when evolution depends on events of mutation that introduce new alleles, mutational and developmental [biases in the introduction of variation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bias_in_the_introduction_of_variation "Bias in the introduction of variation") (arrival biases) can impose biases on evolution without requiring neutral evolution or high mutation rates.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Yampolsky-2001-111)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Stoltzfus-2019-122) Several studies report that the mutations implicated in adaptation reflect common mutation biases[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Stoltzfus-2017-123)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Payne-2019-124)[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Storz-2019-125) though others dispute this interpretation.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Svensson-2019-126)

#### Genetic hitchhiking

Recombination allows alleles on the same strand of DNA to become separated. However, the rate of recombination is low (approximately two events per chromosome per generation). As a result, genes close together on a chromosome may not always be shuffled away from each other and genes that are close together tend to be inherited together, a phenomenon known as [linkage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_linkage "Genetic linkage").[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-127) This tendency is measured by finding how often two alleles occur together on a single chromosome compared to [expectations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independence_(probability_theory) "Independence (probability theory)"), which is called their [linkage disequilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linkage_disequilibrium "Linkage disequilibrium"). A set of alleles that is usually inherited in a group is called a [haplotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haplotype "Haplotype"). This can be important when one allele in a particular haplotype is strongly beneficial: natural selection can drive a [selective sweep](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selective_sweep "Selective sweep") that will also cause the other alleles in the haplotype to become more common in the population; this effect is called genetic hitchhiking or genetic draft.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-128) Genetic draft caused by the fact that some neutral genes are genetically linked to others that are under selection can be partially captured by an appropriate effective population size.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gillespie-2001-96)

#### Sexual selection

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Rana_arvalis2.jpg/250px-Rana_arvalis2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rana_arvalis2.jpg)

Male [moor frogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moor_frog "Moor frog") become blue during the height of mating season. Blue reflectance may be a form of intersexual communication. It is hypothesised that males with brighter blue coloration may signal greater sexual and genetic fitness.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ries-2008-129)

A special case of natural selection is sexual selection, which is selection for any trait that increases mating success by increasing the attractiveness of an organism to potential mates.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-130) Traits that evolved through sexual selection are particularly prominent among males of several animal species. Although sexually favoured, traits such as cumbersome antlers, mating calls, large body size and bright colours often attract predation, which compromises the survival of individual males.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-131)[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Quinn-2001-132) This survival disadvantage is balanced by higher reproductive success in males that show these [hard-to-fake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Handicap_principle "Handicap principle"), sexually selected traits.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-133)

Natural outcomes
----------------

A visual demonstration of rapid [antibiotic resistance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance "Antibiotic resistance") evolution by _E. coli_ growing across a plate with increasing concentrations of [trimethoprim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trimethoprim "Trimethoprim")[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-134)

Evolution influences every aspect of the form and behaviour of organisms. Most prominent are the specific behavioural and physical adaptations that are the outcome of natural selection. These adaptations increase fitness by aiding activities such as finding food, avoiding [predators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predators "Predators") or attracting mates. Organisms can also respond to selection by [cooperating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Co-operation_(evolution) "Co-operation (evolution)") with each other, usually by aiding their relatives or engaging in mutually beneficial [symbiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiosis "Symbiosis"). In the longer term, evolution produces new species through splitting ancestral populations of organisms into new groups that cannot or will not interbreed. These outcomes of evolution are distinguished based on time scale as [macroevolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macroevolution "Macroevolution") versus microevolution. Macroevolution refers to evolution that occurs at or above the level of species, in particular speciation and extinction, whereas microevolution refers to smaller evolutionary changes within a species or population, in particular shifts in allele frequency and adaptation.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Scott-2007-135) Macroevolution is the outcome of long periods of microevolution.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-136) Thus, the distinction between micro- and macroevolution is not a fundamental one—the difference is simply the time involved.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-137) However, in macroevolution, the traits of the entire species may be important. For instance, a large amount of variation among individuals allows a species to rapidly adapt to new [habitats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitat "Habitat"), lessening the chance of it going extinct, while a wide geographic range increases the chance of speciation, by making it more likely that part of the population will become isolated. In this sense, microevolution and macroevolution might involve selection at different levels—with microevolution acting on genes and organisms, versus macroevolutionary processes such as [species selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species_selection "Species selection") acting on entire species and affecting their rates of speciation and extinction.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould2002657%E2%80%93658-138)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gould_1994-139)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Jablonski-2000-140)

A common misconception is that evolution has goals, long-term plans, or an innate tendency for "progress", as expressed in beliefs such as [orthogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogenesis "Orthogenesis") and evolutionism; realistically, however, evolution has no long-term goal and does not necessarily produce greater complexity.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Dougherty-1998-141)[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-142)[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-143) Although [complex species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_of_biological_complexity "Evolution of biological complexity") have evolved, they occur as a side effect of the overall number of organisms increasing, and simple forms of life still remain more common in the biosphere.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Carroll-2001-144) For example, the overwhelming majority of species are microscopic [prokaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prokaryote "Prokaryote"), which form about half the world's [biomass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass_(ecology) "Biomass (ecology)") despite their small size[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-145) and constitute the vast majority of Earth's biodiversity.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Schloss-2004-146) Simple organisms have therefore been the dominant form of life on Earth throughout its history and continue to be the main form of life up to the present day, with complex life only appearing more diverse because it is [more noticeable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sampling_bias "Sampling bias").[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-147) Indeed, the evolution of microorganisms is particularly important to evolutionary research since their rapid reproduction allows the study of [experimental evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experimental_evolution "Experimental evolution") and the observation of evolution and adaptation in real time.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Buckling-2009-148)[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-149)

### Adaptation

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Homology_vertebrates-en.svg/500px-Homology_vertebrates-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homology_vertebrates-en.svg)

[Homologous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homology_(biology) "Homology (biology)") bones in the limbs of [tetrapods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrapod "Tetrapod"). The bones of these animals have the same basic structure, but have been [adapted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adapted "Adapted") for specific uses.[_[image reference needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Adaptation is the process that makes organisms better suited to their habitat.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-150)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-151) Also, the term adaptation may refer to a trait that is important for an organism's survival. For example, the adaptation of horses' teeth to the grinding of grass. By using the term _adaptation_ for the evolutionary process and _adaptive trait_ for the product (the bodily part or function), the two senses of the word may be distinguished. Adaptations are produced by natural selection.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-152) The following definitions are due to Theodosius Dobzhansky:

1.   _Adaptation_ is the evolutionary process whereby an organism becomes better able to live in its habitat or habitats.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-153)
2.   _Adaptedness_ is the state of being adapted: the degree to which an organism is able to live and reproduce in a given set of habitats.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-154)
3.   An _adaptive trait_ is an aspect of the developmental pattern of the organism which enables or enhances the probability of that organism surviving and reproducing.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-155)

Adaptation may cause either the gain of a new feature, or the loss of an ancestral feature. An example that shows both types of change is bacterial adaptation to antibiotic selection, with genetic changes causing antibiotic resistance by both modifying the target of the drug, or increasing the activity of transporters that pump the drug out of the cell.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-156) Other striking examples are the bacteria _[Escherichia coli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escherichia\_coli "Escherichia coli")_ evolving the ability to use [citric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citric_acid "Citric acid") as a nutrient in a [long-term laboratory experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E._coli_long-term_evolution_experiment "E. coli long-term evolution experiment"),[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-157)_[Flavobacterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flavobacterium "Flavobacterium")_ evolving a novel enzyme that allows these bacteria to grow on the by-products of nylon manufacturing,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-158)[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-159) and the soil bacterium _[Sphingobium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphingobium "Sphingobium")_ evolving an entirely new [metabolic pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway") that degrades the synthetic [pesticide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pesticide "Pesticide")[pentachlorophenol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentachlorophenol "Pentachlorophenol").[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-160)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-161) An interesting but still controversial idea is that some adaptations might increase the ability of organisms to generate genetic diversity and adapt by natural selection (increasing organisms' evolvability).[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-162)[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-163)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-164)[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-165)

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Whale_skeleton.png/500px-Whale_skeleton.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Whale_skeleton.png)

A [baleen whale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baleen_whale "Baleen whale") skeleton. Letters _a_ and _b_ label [flipper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy) "Flipper (anatomy)") bones, which were adapted from front leg bones, while _c_ indicates [vestigial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vestigial "Vestigial") leg bones, both suggesting an adaptation from land to sea.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bejder-2002-166)

Adaptation occurs through the gradual modification of existing structures. Consequently, structures with similar internal organisation may have different functions in related organisms. This is the result of a single ancestral structure being adapted to function in different ways. The bones within bat wings, for example, are very similar to those in mice feet and [primate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate "Primate") hands, due to the descent of all these structures from a common mammalian ancestor.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-167) However, since all living organisms are related to some extent,[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Penny-1999-168) even organs that appear to have little or no structural similarity, such as [arthropod](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthropod "Arthropod"), [squid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squid "Squid") and [vertebrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebrate "Vertebrate") eyes, or the limbs and wings of arthropods and vertebrates, can depend on a common set of homologous genes that control their assembly and function; this is called [deep homology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_homology "Deep homology").[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-169)[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-170)

During evolution, some structures may lose their original function and become vestigial structures.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Fong-1995-171) Such structures may have little or no function in a current species, yet have a clear function in ancestral species, or other closely related species. Examples include [pseudogenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudogene "Pseudogene"),[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-172) the non-functional remains of eyes in blind cave-dwelling fish,[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-173) wings in flightless birds,[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-174) the presence of hip bones in whales and snakes,[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bejder-2002-166) and sexual traits in organisms that reproduce via asexual reproduction.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-175) Examples of [vestigial structures in humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_vestigiality "Human vestigiality") include [wisdom teeth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wisdom_teeth "Wisdom teeth"),[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-176) the [coccyx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coccyx "Coccyx"),[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Fong-1995-171) the [vermiform appendix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vermiform_appendix "Vermiform appendix"),[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Fong-1995-171) and other behavioural vestiges such as [goose bumps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goose_bumps "Goose bumps")[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-177)[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-178) and [primitive reflexes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primitive_reflexes "Primitive reflexes").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-179)[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-180)[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-181)

However, many traits that appear to be simple adaptations are in fact [exaptations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exaptation "Exaptation"): structures originally adapted for one function, but which coincidentally became somewhat useful for some other function in the process.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould20021235%E2%80%931236-182) One example is the African lizard _Holaspis guentheri_, which developed an extremely flat head for hiding in crevices, as can be seen by looking at its near relatives. However, in this species, the head has become so flattened that it assists in gliding from tree to tree—an exaptation.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGould20021235%E2%80%931236-182) Within cells, [molecular machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_machine "Molecular machine") such as the bacterial [flagella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flagella "Flagella")[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-183) and [protein sorting machinery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translocase_of_the_inner_membrane "Translocase of the inner membrane")[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-184) evolved by the recruitment of several pre-existing proteins that previously had different functions.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Scott-2007-135) Another example is the recruitment of enzymes from [glycolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycolysis "Glycolysis") and [xenobiotic metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xenobiotic_metabolism "Xenobiotic metabolism") to serve as structural proteins called [crystallins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystallin "Crystallin") within the lenses of organisms' eyes.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-185)[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-186)

An area of current investigation in evolutionary developmental biology is the developmental basis of adaptations and exaptations.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-187) This research addresses the origin and evolution of [embryonic development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embryogenesis "Embryogenesis") and how modifications of development and developmental processes produce novel features.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-188) These studies have shown that evolution can alter development to produce new structures, such as embryonic bone structures that develop into the jaw in other animals instead forming part of the [middle ear in mammals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_of_mammalian_auditory_ossicles "Evolution of mammalian auditory ossicles").[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-189) It is also possible for structures that have been lost in evolution to reappear due to changes in developmental genes, such as a mutation in chickens causing embryos to grow teeth similar to those of crocodiles.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-190) It is now becoming clear that most alterations in the form of organisms are due to changes in a small set of conserved genes.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-191)

### Coevolution

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Thamnophis_sirtalis_sirtalis_Wooster.jpg/250px-Thamnophis_sirtalis_sirtalis_Wooster.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Thamnophis_sirtalis_sirtalis_Wooster.jpg)

The [common garter snake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_garter_snake "Common garter snake") has evolved resistance to the [defensive substance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-predator_adaptation "Anti-predator adaptation")[tetrodotoxin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrodotoxin "Tetrodotoxin") in its amphibian prey.

Interactions between organisms can produce both conflict and cooperation. When the interaction is between pairs of species, such as a [pathogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen") and a [host](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Host_(biology) "Host (biology)"), or a predator and its prey, these species can develop matched sets of adaptations. Here, the evolution of one species causes adaptations in a second species. These changes in the second species then, in turn, cause new adaptations in the first species. This cycle of selection and response is called coevolution.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-192) An example is the production of [tetrodotoxin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrodotoxin "Tetrodotoxin") in the [rough-skinned newt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rough-skinned_newt "Rough-skinned newt") and the evolution of tetrodotoxin resistance in its predator, the [common garter snake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_garter_snake "Common garter snake"). In this predator-prey pair, an [evolutionary arms race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_arms_race "Evolutionary arms race") has produced high levels of toxin in the newt and correspondingly high levels of toxin resistance in the snake.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-193)

### Cooperation

Not all co-evolved interactions between species involve conflict.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-194) Many cases of mutually beneficial interactions have evolved. For instance, an extreme cooperation exists between plants and the [mycorrhizal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mycorrhiza "Mycorrhiza") fungi that grow on their roots and aid the plant in absorbing nutrients from the soil.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-195) This is a [reciprocal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reciprocity_(evolution) "Reciprocity (evolution)") relationship as the plants provide the fungi with sugars from [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis"). Here, the fungi actually grow inside plant cells, allowing them to exchange nutrients with their hosts, while sending [signals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signal_transduction "Signal transduction") that suppress the plant [immune system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system "Immune system").[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-196)

Coalitions between organisms of the same species have also evolved. An extreme case is the [eusociality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eusociality "Eusociality") found in social insects, such as [bees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bee "Bee"), [termites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Termite "Termite") and [ants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ant "Ant"), where sterile insects feed and guard the small number of organisms in a [colony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colony_(biology) "Colony (biology)") that are able to reproduce. On an even smaller scale, the somatic cells that make up the body of an animal limit their reproduction so they can maintain a stable organism, which then supports a small number of the animal's germ cells to produce offspring. Here, somatic cells respond to specific signals that instruct them whether to grow, remain as they are, or die. If cells ignore these signals and multiply inappropriately, their uncontrolled growth [causes cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carcinogenesis "Carcinogenesis").[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bertram-2000-197)

Such cooperation within species may have evolved through the process of [kin selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kin_selection "Kin selection"), which is where one organism acts to help raise a relative's offspring.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-198) This activity is selected for because if the _helping_ individual contains alleles which promote the helping activity, it is likely that its kin will _also_ contain these alleles and thus those alleles will be passed on.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-199) Other processes that may promote cooperation include group selection, where cooperation provides benefits to a group of organisms.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-200)

### Speciation

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Speciation_modes_edit.svg/400px-Speciation_modes_edit.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Speciation_modes_edit.svg)

The four geographic modes of [speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speciation "Speciation")

Speciation is the process where a species diverges into two or more descendant species.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gavrilets-2003-201)

There are multiple ways to define the concept of "species". The choice of definition is dependent on the particularities of the species concerned.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-de_Queiroz-2005-202) For example, some species concepts apply more readily toward sexually reproducing organisms while others lend themselves better toward asexual organisms. Despite the diversity of various species concepts, these various concepts can be placed into one of three broad philosophical approaches: interbreeding, ecological and phylogenetic.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ereshefsky-1992-203) The Biological Species Concept (BSC) is a classic example of the interbreeding approach. Defined by evolutionary biologist [Ernst Mayr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Mayr "Ernst Mayr") in 1942, the BSC states that "species are groups of actually or potentially interbreeding natural populations, which are reproductively isolated from other such groups."[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-204) Despite its wide and long-term use, the BSC like other species concepts is not without controversy, for example, because genetic recombination among prokaryotes is not an intrinsic aspect of reproduction;[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-205) this is called the [species problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species_problem "Species problem").[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-de_Queiroz-2005-202) Some researchers have attempted a unifying monistic definition of species, while others adopt a pluralistic approach and suggest that there may be different ways to logically interpret the definition of a species.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-de_Queiroz-2005-202)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ereshefsky-1992-203)

[Barriers to reproduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproductive_isolation "Reproductive isolation") between two diverging sexual populations are required for the populations to become new species. Gene flow may slow this process by spreading the new genetic variants also to the other populations. Depending on how far two species have diverged since their [most recent common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor "Most recent common ancestor"), it may still be possible for them to produce offspring, as with horses and donkeys mating to produce [mules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mule "Mule").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-206) Such hybrids are generally [infertile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infertile "Infertile"). In this case, closely related species may regularly interbreed, but hybrids will be selected against and the species will remain distinct. However, viable hybrids are occasionally formed and these new species can either have properties intermediate between their parent species, or possess a totally new phenotype.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-207) The importance of hybridisation in producing [new species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_speciation "Hybrid speciation") of animals is unclear, although cases have been seen in many types of animals,[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-208) with the [grey tree frog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grey_tree_frog "Grey tree frog") being a particularly well-studied example.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-209)

Speciation has been observed multiple times under both [controlled laboratory conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laboratory_experiments_of_speciation "Laboratory experiments of speciation") and in nature.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-210) In sexually reproducing organisms, speciation results from reproductive isolation followed by genealogical divergence. There are four primary geographic modes of speciation. The most common in animals is [allopatric speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allopatric_speciation "Allopatric speciation"), which occurs in populations initially isolated geographically, such as by [habitat fragmentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation "Habitat fragmentation") or migration. Selection under these conditions can produce very rapid changes in the appearance and behaviour of organisms.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-211)[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Losos-1997-212) As selection and drift act independently on populations isolated from the rest of their species, separation may eventually produce organisms that cannot interbreed.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-213)

The second mode of speciation is [peripatric speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peripatric_speciation "Peripatric speciation"), which occurs when small populations of organisms become isolated in a new environment. This differs from allopatric speciation in that the isolated populations are numerically much smaller than the parental population. Here, the [founder effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Founder_effect "Founder effect") causes rapid speciation after an increase in [inbreeding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inbreeding "Inbreeding") increases selection on homozygotes, leading to rapid genetic change.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-214)

The third mode is [parapatric speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parapatric_speciation "Parapatric speciation"). This is similar to peripatric speciation in that a small population enters a new habitat, but differs in that there is no physical separation between these two populations. Instead, speciation results from the evolution of mechanisms that reduce gene flow between the two populations.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gavrilets-2003-201) Generally this occurs when there has been a drastic change in the environment within the parental species' habitat.
