One example is the grass _[Anthoxanthum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthoxanthum "Anthoxanthum") odoratum_, which can undergo parapatric speciation in response to localised metal pollution from mines.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-215) Here, plants evolve that have resistance to high levels of metals in the soil. Selection against interbreeding with the metal-sensitive parental population produced a gradual change in the flowering time of the metal-resistant plants, which eventually produced complete reproductive isolation. Selection against hybrids between the two populations may cause [reinforcement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reinforcement_(speciation) "Reinforcement (speciation)"), which is the evolution of traits that promote mating within a species, as well as [character displacement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Character_displacement "Character displacement"), which is when two species become more distinct in appearance.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-216)

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Darwin%27s_finches.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Darwin%27s_finches.jpeg)

[Geographical isolation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographical_isolation "Geographical isolation") of [finches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darwin%27s_finches "Darwin's finches") on the [Galápagos Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands "Galápagos Islands") produced over a dozen new species.

Finally, in [sympatric speciation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sympatric_speciation "Sympatric speciation") species diverge without geographic isolation or changes in habitat. This form is rare since even a small amount of gene flow may remove genetic differences between parts of a population.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-217) Generally, sympatric speciation in animals requires the evolution of both [genetic differences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology) "Polymorphism (biology)") and nonrandom mating, to allow reproductive isolation to evolve.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-218)

One type of sympatric speciation involves [crossbreeding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crossbreed "Crossbreed") of two related species to produce a new hybrid species. This is not common in animals as animal hybrids are usually sterile. This is because during [meiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meiosis "Meiosis") the [homologous chromosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homologous_chromosome "Homologous chromosome") from each parent are from different species and cannot successfully pair. However, it is more common in plants because plants often double their number of chromosomes, to form [polyploids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyploids "Polyploids").[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-219) This allows the chromosomes from each parental species to form matching pairs during meiosis, since each parent's chromosomes are represented by a pair already.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-220) An example of such a speciation event is when the plant species _[Arabidopsis thaliana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabidopsis\_thaliana "Arabidopsis thaliana")_ and _[Arabidopsis arenosa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabidopsis\_arenosa "Arabidopsis arenosa")_ crossbred to give the new species _Arabidopsis suecica_.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-221) This happened about 20,000 years ago,[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-222) and the speciation process has been repeated in the laboratory, which allows the study of the genetic mechanisms involved in this process.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-223) Indeed, chromosome doubling within a species may be a common cause of reproductive isolation, as half the doubled chromosomes will be unmatched when breeding with undoubled organisms.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-S%C3%A9mon-2007-224)

Speciation events are important in the theory of [punctuated equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punctuated_equilibrium "Punctuated equilibrium"), which accounts for the pattern in the fossil record of short "bursts" of evolution interspersed with relatively long periods of stasis, where species remain relatively unchanged.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-225) In this theory, speciation and [rapid evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Contemporary_evolution&action=edit&redlink=1 "Contemporary evolution (page does not exist)") are linked, with natural selection and genetic drift acting most strongly on organisms undergoing speciation in novel habitats or small populations. As a result, the periods of stasis in the fossil record correspond to the parental population and the organisms undergoing speciation and rapid evolution are found in small populations or geographically restricted habitats and therefore rarely being preserved as fossils.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gould_1994-139)

### Extinction

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Palais_de_la_Decouverte_Tyrannosaurus_rex_p1050042.jpg/250px-Palais_de_la_Decouverte_Tyrannosaurus_rex_p1050042.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Palais_de_la_Decouverte_Tyrannosaurus_rex_p1050042.jpg)

_[Tyrannosaurus rex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrannosaurus\_rex "Tyrannosaurus rex")_. Non-[avian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird "Bird") dinosaurs died out in the [Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event "Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event") at the end of the [Cretaceous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous "Cretaceous") period.

Extinction is the disappearance of an entire species. Extinction is not an unusual event, as species regularly appear through speciation and disappear through extinction.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-226) Nearly all animal and plant species that have lived on Earth are now extinct,[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-227) and extinction appears to be the ultimate fate of all species.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-228) These extinctions have happened continuously throughout the history of life, although the rate of extinction spikes in occasional mass [extinction events](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction_event "Extinction event").[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Raup-1994-229) The [Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event "Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event"), during which the non-avian dinosaurs became extinct, is the most well-known, but the earlier [Permian–Triassic extinction event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event "Permian–Triassic extinction event") was even more severe, with approximately 96% of all marine species driven to extinction.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Raup-1994-229) The [Holocene extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holocene_extinction "Holocene extinction") event is an ongoing mass extinction associated with humanity's expansion across the globe over the past few thousand years. Present-day extinction rates are 100–1000 times greater than the background rate and up to 30% of current species may be extinct by the mid 21st century.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-230) Human activities are now the primary cause of the ongoing extinction event;[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-231)[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-232)[global warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming "Global warming") may further accelerate it in the future.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-233) Despite the estimated extinction of more than 99% of all species that ever lived on Earth,[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Stearns-1999-234)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Novacek-2014-235) about 1 trillion species are estimated to be on Earth currently with only one-thousandth of 1% described.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-NSF-2016-236)

The role of extinction in evolution is not very well understood and may depend on which type of extinction is considered.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Raup-1994-229) The causes of the continuous "low-level" extinction events, which form the majority of extinctions, may be the result of competition between species for limited resources (the [competitive exclusion principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Competitive_exclusion_principle "Competitive exclusion principle")).[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kutschera-2004-237) If one species can out-compete another, this could produce species selection, with the fitter species surviving and the other species being driven to extinction.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Gould-1998-84) The intermittent mass extinctions are also important, but instead of acting as a selective force, they drastically reduce diversity in a nonspecific manner and promote bursts of rapid evolution and speciation in survivors.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-238)

Applications
------------

Concepts and models used in evolutionary biology, such as natural selection, have many applications.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bull-2001-239)

Artificial selection is the intentional selection of traits in a population of organisms. This has been used for thousands of years in the [domestication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication "Domestication") of plants and animals.[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-240) More recently, such selection has become a vital part of [genetic engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_engineering "Genetic engineering"), with [selectable markers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selectable_marker "Selectable marker") such as antibiotic resistance genes being used to manipulate DNA. Proteins with valuable properties have evolved by repeated rounds of mutation and selection (for example modified enzymes and new [antibodies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibodies "Antibodies")) in a process called [directed evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_evolution "Directed evolution").[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-241)

Understanding the changes that have occurred during an organism's evolution can reveal the genes needed to construct parts of the body, genes which may be involved in human [genetic disorders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_disorder "Genetic disorder").[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-242) For example, the [Mexican tetra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexican_tetra "Mexican tetra") is an [albino](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albino "Albino") cavefish that lost its eyesight during evolution. Breeding together different populations of this blind fish produced some offspring with functional eyes, since different mutations had occurred in the isolated populations that had evolved in different caves.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-243) This helped identify genes required for vision and pigmentation.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-244)

Evolutionary theory has many [applications in medicine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_therapy "Evolutionary therapy"). Many human diseases are not static phenomena, but capable of evolution. Viruses, bacteria, fungi and cancers evolve to be resistant to host immune defences, as well as to [pharmaceutical drugs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug "Pharmaceutical drug").[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-245)[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-246)[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-247) These same problems occur in agriculture with pesticide[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-248) and [herbicide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbicide "Herbicide")[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-249) resistance. It is possible that we are facing the end of the effective life of most of available antibiotics[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-250) and predicting the evolution and evolvability[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-251) of our pathogens and devising strategies to slow or circumvent it is requiring deeper knowledge of the complex forces driving evolution at the molecular level.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-252)

In [computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science"), simulations of evolution using [evolutionary algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithm "Evolutionary algorithm") and [artificial life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_life "Artificial life") started in the 1960s and were extended with simulation of artificial selection.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-253) Artificial evolution became a widely recognised optimisation method as a result of the work of [Ingo Rechenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ingo_Rechenberg "Ingo Rechenberg") in the 1960s. He used [evolution strategies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_strategies "Evolution strategies") to solve complex engineering problems.[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-254)[Genetic algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_algorithm "Genetic algorithm") in particular became popular through the writing of [John Henry Holland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Henry_Holland "John Henry Holland").[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-255) Practical applications also include [automatic evolution of computer programmes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_programming "Genetic programming").[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-256) Evolutionary algorithms are now used to solve multi-dimensional problems more efficiently than software produced by human designers and also to optimise the design of systems.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-257)

Evolutionary history of life
----------------------------

### Origin of life

The Earth is [about 4.54 billion years old](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth").[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-USGS-2007-258)[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Dalrymple-2001-259)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Manhesa-1980-260) The earliest undisputed evidence of life on Earth dates from at least 3.5 billion years ago,[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Schopf-2007-12)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-261) during the [Eoarchean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eoarchean "Eoarchean") Era after a geological [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crust_(geology) "Crust (geology)") started to solidify following the earlier molten [Hadean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadean "Hadean") Eon. Microbial mat fossils have been found in 3.48 billion-year-old sandstone in Western Australia.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Borenstein-2013-14)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Pearlman-2013-15)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Noffke-2013-16) Other early physical evidence of a biogenic substance is graphite in 3.7 billion-year-old [metasedimentary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metasediment "Metasediment") rocks discovered in Western Greenland[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ohtomo-2014-13) as well as "remains of [biotic life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotic_life "Biotic life")" found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Borenstein-2015-262)[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bell-2015-263) Commenting on the Australian findings, [Stephen Blair Hedges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Blair_Hedges "Stephen Blair Hedges") wrote: "If life arose relatively quickly on Earth, then it could be common in the universe."[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Borenstein-2015-262)[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-264) In July 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 [genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") from the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") (LUCA) of all organisms living on Earth.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Wade-2016-265)

More than 99% of all species, amounting to over five billion species,[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Book-Biology-266) that ever lived on Earth are estimated to be extinct.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Stearns-1999-234)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Novacek-2014-235) Estimates on the number of Earth's current species range from 10 million to 14 million,[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Mora-2011-267)[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Miller-268) of which about 1.9 million are estimated to have been named[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Chapman2009-269) and 1.6 million documented in a central database to date,[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Roskov-2016-270) leaving at least 80% not yet described.

Highly energetic chemistry is thought to have produced a self-replicating molecule around 4 billion years ago, and half a billion years later the last common ancestor of all life existed.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Doolittle-2000-10) The current scientific consensus is that the complex biochemistry that makes up life came from simpler chemical reactions.[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-271)[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-BBC-20201111-272) The beginning of life may have included self-replicating molecules such as [RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA "RNA")[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-273) and the assembly of simple cells.[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-274)

### Common descent

All organisms on Earth are descended from a common ancestor or ancestral [gene pool](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_pool "Gene pool").[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Penny-1999-168)[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-275) Current species are a stage in the process of evolution, with their diversity the product of a long series of speciation and extinction events.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-276) The common descent of organisms was first deduced from four simple facts about organisms: First, they have geographic distributions that cannot be explained by local adaptation. Second, the diversity of life is not a set of completely unique organisms, but organisms that share morphological similarities. Third, [vestigial traits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vestigial_trait "Vestigial trait") with no clear purpose resemble functional ancestral traits. Fourth, organisms can be classified using these similarities into a hierarchy of nested groups, similar to a family tree.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-277)

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Ape_skeletons.png/500px-Ape_skeletons.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ape_skeletons.png)

The [hominoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominoids "Hominoids") are descendants of a [common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_ancestor "Common ancestor").

Due to horizontal gene transfer, this "tree of life" may be more complicated than a simple branching tree, since some genes have spread independently between distantly related species.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-278)[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-279) To solve this problem and others, some authors prefer to use the "[Coral of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral_of_life "Coral of life")" as a metaphor or a mathematical model to illustrate the evolution of life. This view dates back to an idea briefly mentioned by Darwin but later abandoned.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bnotebook-280)

Past species have also left records of their evolutionary history. Fossils, along with the comparative anatomy of present-day organisms, constitute the morphological, or anatomical, record.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Jablonski-1999-281) By comparing the anatomies of both modern and extinct species, palaeontologists can infer the lineages of those species. However, this approach is most successful for organisms that had hard body parts, such as shells, bones or teeth. Further, as prokaryotes such as bacteria and archaea share a limited set of common morphologies, their fossils do not provide information on their ancestry.

More recently, evidence for common descent has come from the study of biochemical similarities between organisms. For example, all living cells use the same basic set of nucleotides and [amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid").[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-282) The development of [molecular genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_genetics "Molecular genetics") has revealed the record of evolution left in organisms' genomes: dating when species diverged through the [molecular clock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_clock "Molecular clock") produced by mutations.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-283) For example, these DNA sequence comparisons have revealed that humans and chimpanzees share 98% of their genomes and analysing the few areas where they differ helps shed light on when the common ancestor of these species existed.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-284)

### Evolution of life

![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/330px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png)

[Evolutionary tree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_tree "Evolutionary tree") showing the divergence of modern species from their common ancestor in the centre.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ciccarelli-2006-285) The three [domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(biology) "Domain (biology)") are coloured, with bacteria blue, [archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea") green and [eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote") red.

Prokaryotes inhabited the Earth from approximately 3–4 billion years ago.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Cavalier-Smith-2006-286)[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-287) No obvious changes in morphology or cellular organisation occurred in these organisms over the next few billion years.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-288) The eukaryotic cells emerged between 1.6 and 2.7 billion years ago. The next major change in cell structure came when bacteria were engulfed by eukaryotic cells, in a cooperative association called [endosymbiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endosymbiosis "Endosymbiosis").[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Poole-2007-289)[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Dyall-2004-290) The engulfed bacteria and the host cell then underwent coevolution, with the bacteria evolving into either mitochondria or [hydrogenosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenosome "Hydrogenosome").[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-291) Another engulfment of [cyanobacterial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanobacteria "Cyanobacteria")-like organisms led to the formation of chloroplasts in algae and plants.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-292)

The history of life was that of the [unicellular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unicellular "Unicellular") eukaryotes, prokaryotes and archaea until around 1.7 billion years ago, when [multicellular organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multicellular_organism "Multicellular organism") began to appear, with [differentiated cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellular_differentiation "Cellular differentiation") performing specialised functions.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Bonner1999-293) The [evolution of multicellularity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_of_multicellularity "Evolution of multicellularity") occurred in multiple independent events, in organisms as diverse as [sponges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sponge "Sponge"), [brown algae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_algae "Brown algae"), cyanobacteria, [slime moulds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slime_moulds "Slime moulds") and [myxobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myxobacteria "Myxobacteria").[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-294) In January 2016, scientists reported that, about 800 million years ago, a minor genetic change in a single molecule called GK-PID may have allowed organisms to go from a single cell organism to one of many cells.[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Zimmer-2016-295)

Approximately 538.8 million years ago, a remarkable amount of biological diversity appeared over a span of around 10 million years in what is called the [Cambrian explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambrian_explosion "Cambrian explosion"). Here, the majority of [types](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylum "Phylum") of modern animals appeared in the fossil record, as well as unique lineages that subsequently became extinct.[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Valentine-1999-296) Various triggers for the Cambrian explosion have been proposed, including the accumulation of oxygen in the atmosphere from photosynthesis.[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-297)

About 500 million years ago, plants and fungi colonised the land and were soon followed by arthropods and other animals.[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-298) Insects were particularly successful and even today make up the majority of animal species.[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-299)[Amphibians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian "Amphibian") first appeared around 364 million years ago, followed by early [amniotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amniote "Amniote") and birds around 155 million years ago (both from "reptile"-like lineages), [mammals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal "Mammal") around 129 million years ago, [Homininae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homininae "Homininae") around 10 million years ago and [modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans "Anatomically modern humans") around 250,000 years ago.[[300]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-300)[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-301)[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-302) However, despite the evolution of these large animals, smaller organisms similar to the types that evolved early in this process continue to be highly successful and dominate the Earth, with the majority of both biomass and species being prokaryotes.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Schloss-2004-146)

History of evolutionary thought
-------------------------------

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Lucretius_Rome.jpg/250px-Lucretius_Rome.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lucretius_Rome.jpg)

[Lucretius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucretius "Lucretius")

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Alfred-Russel-Wallace-c1895.jpg/250px-Alfred-Russel-Wallace-c1895.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alfred-Russel-Wallace-c1895.jpg)

[Alfred Russel Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace "Alfred Russel Wallace")

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Thomas_Robert_Malthus_Wellcome_L0069037_-crop.jpg/250px-Thomas_Robert_Malthus_Wellcome_L0069037_-crop.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Thomas_Robert_Malthus_Wellcome_L0069037_-crop.jpg)

[Thomas Robert Malthus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Robert_Malthus "Thomas Robert Malthus")

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Charles_Darwin_aged_51.jpg/250px-Charles_Darwin_aged_51.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Charles_Darwin_aged_51.jpg)

In 1842, [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin") penned his first sketch of _[On the Origin of Species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On\_the\_Origin\_of\_Species "On the Origin of Species")_.[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-303)

### Classical antiquity

The proposal that one type of organism could descend from another type goes back to some of the first [pre-Socratic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Socratic "Pre-Socratic") Greek philosophers, such as [Anaximander](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaximander#Origin_of_humankind "Anaximander") and [Empedocles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empedocles#Cosmogony "Empedocles").[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-304) Such proposals survived into Roman times. The poet and philosopher [Lucretius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucretius "Lucretius") followed Empedocles in his masterwork _[De rerum natura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De\_rerum\_natura "De rerum natura")_ (lit.'On the Nature of Things').[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-305)[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-306)

### Middle Ages

In contrast to these [materialistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materialism "Materialism") views, [Aristotelianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotelianism "Aristotelianism") had considered all natural things as [actualisations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality "Potentiality and actuality") of fixed natural possibilities, known as [forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_forms "Theory of forms").[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Torrey-1937-307)[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hull-1967-308) This became part of a medieval [teleological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleological "Teleological") understanding of [nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nature_(philosophy) "Nature (philosophy)") in which all things have an intended role to play in a [divine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divine "Divine")[cosmic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmos "Cosmos") order. Variations of this idea became the standard understanding of the [Middle Ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Ages "Middle Ages") and were integrated into Christian learning, but Aristotle did not demand that real types of organisms always correspond one-for-one with exact metaphysical forms and specifically gave examples of how new types of living things could come to be.[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-309)

A number of Arab Muslim scholars wrote about evolution, most notably [Ibn Khaldun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun "Ibn Khaldun"), who wrote the book _[Muqaddimah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muqaddimah "Muqaddimah")_ in 1377, in which he asserted that humans developed from "the world of the monkeys", in a process by which "species become more numerous".[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kiros-2001-310)

### Pre-Darwinian

The ["New Science"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_revolution "Scientific revolution") of the 17th century rejected the Aristotelian approach. It sought to explain natural phenomena in terms of [physical laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_law "Physical law") that were the same for all visible things and that did not require the existence of any fixed natural categories or divine cosmic order. However, this new approach was slow to take root in the biological sciences: the last bastion of the concept of fixed natural types. [John Ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ray "John Ray") applied one of the previously more general terms for fixed natural types, "species", to plant and animal types, but he strictly identified each type of living thing as a species and proposed that each species could be defined by the features that perpetuated themselves generation after generation.[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-311) The [biological classification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_classification "Biological classification") introduced by [Carl Linnaeus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus "Carl Linnaeus") in 1735 explicitly recognised the hierarchical nature of species relationships, but still viewed species as fixed according to a divine plan.[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-312)

Other [naturalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalists "Naturalists") of this time speculated on the evolutionary change of species over time according to natural laws. In 1751, [Pierre Louis Maupertuis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Maupertuis "Pierre Louis Maupertuis") wrote of natural modifications occurring during reproduction and accumulating over many generations to produce new species.[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-313)[Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon "Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon"), suggested that species could degenerate into different organisms, and [Erasmus Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin "Erasmus Darwin") proposed that all warm-blooded animals could have descended from a single microorganism (or "filament").[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-314) The first full-fledged evolutionary scheme was [Jean-Baptiste Lamarck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck "Jean-Baptiste Lamarck")'s "transmutation" theory of 1809,[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-315) which envisaged [spontaneous generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_generation "Spontaneous generation") continually producing simple forms of life that developed greater complexity in parallel lineages with an inherent progressive tendency, and postulated that on a local level, these lineages adapted to the environment by inheriting changes caused by their use or disuse in parents.[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Nardon_Grenier91-316) (The latter process was later called [Lamarckism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamarckism "Lamarckism").)[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Nardon_Grenier91-316)[[317]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ghiselin-1994-317)[[318]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Jablonka-2007-318) These ideas were condemned by established naturalists as speculation lacking empirical support. In particular, [Georges Cuvier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georges_Cuvier "Georges Cuvier") insisted that species were unrelated and fixed, their similarities reflecting divine design for functional needs. In the meantime, Ray's ideas of benevolent design had been developed by [William Paley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Paley "William Paley") into the _[Natural Theology or Evidences of the Existence and Attributes of the Deity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural\_Theology\_or\_Evidences\_of\_the\_Existence\_and\_Attributes\_of\_the\_Deity "Natural Theology or Evidences of the Existence and Attributes of the Deity")_ (1802), which proposed complex adaptations as evidence of divine design and which was admired by Charles Darwin.[[319]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Darwin91-319)[[320]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Sulloway-2009-320)

### Darwinian revolution

The crucial break from the concept of constant typological classes or types in biology came with the theory of evolution through natural selection, which was formulated by [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin") and [Alfred Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Wallace "Alfred Wallace") in terms of variable populations. Darwin used the expression _descent with modification_ rather than _evolution_.[[321]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-321) Partly influenced by _[An Essay on the Principle of Population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/An\_Essay\_on\_the\_Principle\_of\_Population "An Essay on the Principle of Population")_ (1798) by [Thomas Robert Malthus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Robert_Malthus "Thomas Robert Malthus"), Darwin noted that population growth would lead to a "struggle for existence" in which favourable variations prevailed as others perished. In each generation, many offspring fail to survive to an age of reproduction because of limited resources. This could explain the diversity of plants and animals from a common ancestry through the working of natural laws in the same way for all types of organism.[[322]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Sober-2009-322)[[323]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-323)[[324]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-324)[[325]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-325) Darwin developed his theory of "natural selection" from 1838 onwards and was writing up his "big book" on the subject when [Alfred Russel Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace "Alfred Russel Wallace") sent him a version of virtually the same theory in 1858. Their [separate papers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On_the_Tendency_of_Species_to_form_Varieties;_and_on_the_Perpetuation_of_Varieties_and_Species_by_Natural_Means_of_Selection "On the Tendency of Species to form Varieties; and on the Perpetuation of Varieties and Species by Natural Means of Selection") were presented together at an 1858 meeting of the [Linnean Society of London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linnean_Society_of_London "Linnean Society of London").[[326]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-326) At the end of 1859, Darwin's publication of his "abstract" as _On the Origin of Species_ explained natural selection in detail and in a way that led to an increasingly wide acceptance of [Darwin's concepts of evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darwinism "Darwinism") at the expense of [alternative theories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternatives_to_evolution_by_natural_selection "Alternatives to evolution by natural selection"). [Thomas Henry Huxley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley "Thomas Henry Huxley") applied Darwin's ideas to humans, using [palaeontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palaeontology "Palaeontology") and [comparative anatomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comparative_anatomy "Comparative anatomy") to provide strong evidence that humans and [apes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ape "Ape") shared a common ancestry. Some were disturbed by this since it implied that humans did not have a special place in the [universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe").[[327]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-327)

[Othniel C. Marsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Othniel_C._Marsh "Othniel C. Marsh"), America's first palaeontologist, was the first to provide solid fossil evidence to support Darwin's theory of evolution by unearthing the ancestors of the modern horse.[[328]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-328) In 1877, Marsh delivered a very influential speech before the annual meeting of the American Association for the Advancement of Science, providing a demonstrative argument for evolution. For the first time, Marsh traced the evolution of vertebrates from fish all the way through humans. Sparing no detail, he listed a wealth of fossil examples of past life forms. The significance of this speech was immediately recognised by the scientific community, and it was printed in its entirety in several scientific journals.[[329]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-329)[[330]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-330)

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Hesperornis_Regalis_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16474.jpg/250px-Hesperornis_Regalis_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16474.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hesperornis_Regalis_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_16474.jpg)

_Hesperornis regalis,_ a species of ancient flightless bird with teeth, as drawn by [Othniel Marsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh "Othniel Charles Marsh"), and published in his book, _Odontornithes: A Monograph on the Extinct Toothed Birds of North America._

In 1880, Marsh caught the attention of the scientific world with the publication of _Odontornithes: a Monograph on Extinct Birds of North America,_ which included his discoveries of birds with teeth. These skeletons helped bridge the gap between dinosaurs and birds, and provided invaluable support for Darwin's theory of evolution.[[331]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarren199316%E2%80%9317-331) Darwin wrote to Marsh saying, "Your work on these old birds & on the many fossil animals of N. America has afforded the best support to the theory of evolution, which has appeared within the last 20 years" (since Darwin's publication of _Origin of Species).[[332]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite\_note-332)[[333]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite\_note-333)_

### Pangenesis and heredity

The mechanisms of reproductive heritability and the origin of new traits remained a mystery. Towards this end, Darwin developed his provisional theory of [pangenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pangenesis "Pangenesis").[[334]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Liu-2009-334) In 1865, [Gregor Mendel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregor_Mendel "Gregor Mendel") reported that traits were inherited in a predictable manner through the [independent assortment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_assortment "Independent assortment") and segregation of elements (later known as genes). Mendel's laws of inheritance eventually supplanted most of Darwin's pangenesis theory.[[335]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Weiling-1991-335)[August Weismann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_Weismann "August Weismann") made the important distinction between [germ cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germ_cell "Germ cell") that give rise to [gametes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamete "Gamete") (such as [sperm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sperm "Sperm") and [egg cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg_cell "Egg cell")) and the [somatic cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Somatic_cell "Somatic cell") of the body, demonstrating that heredity passes through the germ line only. [Hugo de Vries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugo_de_Vries "Hugo de Vries") connected Darwin's pangenesis theory to Weismann's germ/soma cell distinction and proposed that Darwin's pangenes were concentrated in the [cell nucleus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_nucleus "Cell nucleus") and when expressed they could move into the [cytoplasm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytoplasm "Cytoplasm") to change the [cell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology) "Cell (biology)")'s structure. De Vries was also one of the researchers who made Mendel's work well known, believing that Mendelian traits corresponded to the transfer of heritable variations along the germline.[[336]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Wright84-336) To explain how new variants originate, de Vries developed [a mutation theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutationism "Mutationism") that led to a temporary rift between those who accepted Darwinian evolution and biometricians who allied with de Vries.[[337]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-337)[[338]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-338) In the 1930s, pioneers in the field of [population genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_genetics "Population genetics"), such as [Ronald Fisher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Fisher "Ronald Fisher"), [Sewall Wright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sewall_Wright "Sewall Wright") and [J. B. S. Haldane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._B._S._Haldane "J. B. S. Haldane") set the foundations of evolution onto a robust statistical philosophy. The false contradiction between Darwin's theory, genetic mutations, and [Mendelian inheritance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance "Mendelian inheritance") was thus reconciled.[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowler1989307%E2%80%93318-339)

### The 'modern synthesis'

In the 1920s and 1930s, the [modern synthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_synthesis "Modern synthesis") connected natural selection and population genetics, based on Mendelian inheritance, into a unified theory that included random genetic drift, mutation, and gene flow. This new version of evolutionary theory focused on changes in allele frequencies in population. It explained patterns observed across species in populations, through [fossil transitions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transitional_fossil "Transitional fossil") in palaeontology.[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowler1989307%E2%80%93318-339)

### Further syntheses

Since then, further syntheses have extended evolution's explanatory power in the light of numerous discoveries, to cover biological phenomena across the whole of the [biological hierarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_organisation "Biological organisation") from genes to populations.[[340]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevinson2019-340)

The publication of the structure of [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") by [James Watson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Watson "James Watson") and [Francis Crick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Crick "Francis Crick") with contribution of [Rosalind Franklin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin "Rosalind Franklin") in 1953 demonstrated a physical mechanism for inheritance.[[341]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Watson-1953-341)[Molecular biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_biology "Molecular biology") improved understanding of the relationship between [genotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genotype "Genotype") and [phenotype](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotype "Phenotype"). Advances were also made in phylogenetic [systematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Systematics "Systematics"), mapping the transition of traits into a comparative and testable framework through the publication and use of [evolutionary trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_trees "Evolutionary trees").[[342]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Hennig99-342) In 1973, evolutionary biologist [Theodosius Dobzhansky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodosius_Dobzhansky "Theodosius Dobzhansky") penned that "[nothing in biology makes sense except in the light of evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nothing_in_biology_makes_sense_except_in_the_light_of_evolution "Nothing in biology makes sense except in the light of evolution")", because it has brought to light the relations of what first seemed disjointed facts in natural history into a coherent [explanatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Explanatory "Explanatory") body of knowledge that describes and predicts many observable facts about life on this planet.[[343]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Dobzhansky-1973-343)

One extension, known as [evolutionary developmental biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology "Evolutionary developmental biology") and informally called "evo-devo", emphasises how changes between generations (evolution) act on patterns of change within individual organisms ([development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developmental_biology "Developmental biology")).[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kutschera-2004-237)[[344]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Avise10-344) Since the beginning of the 21st century, some biologists have argued for an [extended evolutionary synthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extended_evolutionary_synthesis "Extended evolutionary synthesis"), which would account for the effects of non-genetic inheritance modes, such as [epigenetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epigenetics "Epigenetics"), [parental effects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maternal_effect "Maternal effect"), ecological inheritance and [cultural inheritance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_inheritance_theory "Dual inheritance theory"), and [evolvability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolvability "Evolvability").[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Danchin-2011-345)[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-eesbook-346)

Social and cultural responses
-----------------------------

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Editorial_cartoon_depicting_Charles_Darwin_as_an_ape_%281871%29.jpg/250px-Editorial_cartoon_depicting_Charles_Darwin_as_an_ape_%281871%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Editorial_cartoon_depicting_Charles_Darwin_as_an_ape_(1871).jpg)

As evolution became widely accepted in the 1870s, [caricatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caricature "Caricature") of Charles Darwin with an [ape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ape "Ape") or monkey body symbolised evolution.[[347]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-347)

In the 19th century, particularly after the publication of _On the Origin of Species_ in 1859, the idea that life had evolved was an active source of academic debate centred on the philosophical, social and religious implications of evolution. Today, the modern evolutionary synthesis is accepted by a vast majority of scientists.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Kutschera-2004-237) However, evolution remains a contentious concept for some [theists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theists "Theists").[[348]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-348)

While [various religions and denominations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Level_of_support_for_evolution#Religious "Level of support for evolution") have reconciled their beliefs with evolution through concepts such as [theistic evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theistic_evolution "Theistic evolution"), there are [creationists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creationists "Creationists") who believe that evolution is contradicted by the [creation myths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creation_myth "Creation myth") found in their religions and who raise various [objections to evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Objections_to_evolution "Objections to evolution").[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Scott-2007-135)[[349]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Ross-2005-349)[[350]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-350) As had been demonstrated by responses to the publication of _[Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vestiges\_of\_the\_Natural\_History\_of\_Creation "Vestiges of the Natural History of Creation")_ in 1844, the most controversial aspect of evolutionary biology is the implication of [human evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution "Human evolution") that humans share common ancestry with apes and that the mental and [moral faculties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_of_morality "Evolution of morality") of humanity have the same types of natural causes as other inherited traits in animals.[[351]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-351) In some countries, notably the United States, these tensions between science and religion have fuelled the current creation–evolution controversy, a religious conflict focusing on politics and [public education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creation_and_evolution_in_public_education "Creation and evolution in public education").[[352]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-352) While other scientific fields such as [cosmology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_cosmology "Physical cosmology")[[353]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Spergel-2003-353) and [Earth science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_science "Earth science")[[354]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Wilde-2001-354) also conflict with literal interpretations of many [religious texts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_text "Religious text"), evolutionary biology experiences significantly more opposition from religious literalists.

The teaching of evolution in American secondary school biology classes was uncommon in most of the first half of the 20th century. The [Scopes trial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scopes_trial "Scopes trial") decision of 1925 caused the subject to become very rare in American secondary biology textbooks for a generation, but it was gradually re-introduced later and became legally protected with the 1968 _[Epperson v. Arkansas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epperson\_v.\_Arkansas "Epperson v. Arkansas")_ decision. Since then, the competing religious belief of creationism was legally disallowed in secondary school curricula in various decisions in the 1970s and 1980s, but it returned in [pseudoscientific](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudoscientific "Pseudoscientific") form as [intelligent design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligent_design "Intelligent design") (ID), to be excluded once again in the 2005 _[Kitzmiller v. Dover Area School District](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kitzmiller\_v.\_Dover\_Area\_School\_District "Kitzmiller v. Dover Area School District")_ case.[[355]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Branch-2007-355) The debate over Darwin's ideas did not generate significant controversy in China.[[356]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution#cite_note-Xiaoxing-2019-356)
